The Foundling: Rulings on Picking Up, Status, and Maintenance
Defines laqit, ruling on picking up, free status, and maintenance from bayt al-mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on picking up a foundling, explain why the foundling is free, and identify the source of his maintenance.

Core Idea
A foundling is free; his maintenance is from the public treasury; picking him up is recommended but obligatory if his life is in danger.

This passage opens Kitab al-Laqit in the Hidayah, establishing the foundational rulings for foundlings (laqit). It addresses three core issues: the legal nature of picking up a foundling, the presumption of freedom, and the financial responsibility for his care. Understanding these principles is essential for anyone dealing with abandoned children within the Hanafi legal framework.

Guided Path
1
Define the foundling and the act of picking up
Identify the term 'laqit' and the act 'iltiqat'. Note that the child is abandoned and lineage unknown.
2
Identify the ruling on iltiqat
Determine when picking up is mandub (recommended) and when it becomes wajib (obligatory). The key factor is the observer's strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the child will die otherwise.
The ruling changes based on risk to life.
3
Explain the foundling's free status
State that the foundling is free by default. The proof is the principle that the original state of humans is freedom, and Dar al-Islam is predominantly a land of free people.
4
Determine the source of maintenance
The foundling's nafaqah is from bayt al-mal (public treasury). This is established by the practice of Caliphs Umar and Ali and by qiyas to a paralyzed person with no wealth.
The qiyas uses the common illa: inability to earn, lack of wealth, lack of relatives.
5
Understand the multiqit's role
The finder is a volunteer (mutabarri) unless the judge orders him, making it a debt on him. This preserves the principle of guardianship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب اللقيط

اللقيط سُمّي به باعتبار ماله لما أنه يلقط والالتقاط مندوب إليه لما فيه إحياؤه وإن غلب على ظنه ضياعه فواجب

قال اللقيط حر لأن الأصل في بني آدم إنما هو الحرية وكذا الدار دار الأحرار ولأن الحكم للغالب ونفقته في بيت المال هو المروي عن عمر وعلي ولأنه مسلم عاجز عن التكسب ولا مال له ولا قرابة فأشبه المقعد الذي لا مال له ولأن ميراثه لبيت المال والخراج بالضمان ولهذا كانت جنايته فيه والملتقط متبرع في الإنفاق عليه لعدم الولاية إلا أن يأمره القاضي به ليكون دينا عليه لعموم الولاية
The foundling is named because of what pertains to him, since he is picked up. Picking him up is recommended because it preserves his life, and if his loss dominates his assumption, it becomes obligatory. He said: The foundling is free because the original principle in the children of Adam is freedom, and likewise the land is the land of free people, and because the ruling is based on the predominant. His maintenance is from the public treasury, which is narrated from Umar and Ali. And because he is a Muslim unable to earn, has no wealth and no relatives, so he resembles the paralyzed person who has no wealth. And because his inheritance is for the public treasury, and revenue is based on responsibility; therefore his crime is against it. And the picker-upper is a volunteer in spending on him due to lack of guardianship, unless the judge orders him to do so so that it becomes a debt on him due to the generality of guardianship.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This is the opening of Kitab al-Laqit in the Hidayah. The passage defines the foundling (laqit) and gives the ruling on picking him up. The act of picking up (iltiqat) is mandub (recommended) because it preserves life, but becomes wajib (obligatory) if the observer has a strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the child will die without intervention. The foundling is declared free (hurr) based on the principle that the default in humans is freedom, and because Dar al-Islam is predominantly a land of free people. His maintenance (nafaqah) is from the public treasury (bayt al-mal), as established by the practice of Caliphs Umar and Ali (radiyallahu anhuma). The text draws an analogy to a paralyzed person with no wealth, whose maintenance also falls on the treasury. The foundling's inheritance goes to the bayt al-mal, and if he commits a crime, the liability is borne by the treasury (aligning with the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'). The person who picks him up (multaqit) is considered a volunteer in spending, lacking guardianship, unless the judge orders him to pay, in which case it becomes a debt on him. This establishes the foundational legal status of foundlings in the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
لقيط (laqit): A foundling: a child found abandoned and whose lineage is unknown.
التقاط (iltiqat): The act of picking up a foundling from the ground.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Islamic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does picking up a foundling become obligatory (wajib)?
A) Always
B) When the observer has a strong presumption that the child will die if not picked up
C) When the child is found near a mosque
D) Only if the judge orders it

Key Takeaway
A foundling is free, his maintenance is from the public treasury, and picking him up is recommended but obligatory if his life is in danger.

Expenses of a Foundling and the Judge's Authorization
Ruling on foundling expenses from treasury and effect of judge's order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the expenses of a foundling and explain when the finder's spending becomes a recoverable loan.

Core Idea
The foundling's expenses are from the treasury; the finder's spending is a favor unless the judge explicitly orders it as a loan.

When a foundling is taken in by someone, questions arise about who bears the cost of upbringing. Hanafi fiqh provides a clear default rule: the public treasury is responsible. The finder, however, may spend out of kindness, but unless the judge gives a specific order declaring those expenses a loan, the finder cannot recover them later. This lesson unpacks the conditions under which the finder's spending becomes a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source of expenses
State that the foundling's expenses are primarily covered by the public treasury (Bait al-Mal).
This is the default obligation of the Islamic state.
2
Understand the finder's default position
The finder (multaqit) has no legal authority over the foundling, so any spending is considered a voluntary favor (ehsan).
Without authority, the finder cannot compel repayment.
3
Recognize the exception with judge's order
If the judge (qadi) explicitly orders the finder to spend and declares it a loan (qarz), then the amount becomes recoverable from the foundling upon adulthood.
The judge's general guardianship allows him to create this obligation.
4
Differentiate between mere order and loan declaration
A simple court order to care for the child does not create a loan; only the judge's explicit statement that the spending is a loan makes it recoverable.
This is the relied-upon opinion (asahh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کا خرچ بیت المال سے ہی پورا کیا جاتا ہے ۔ اس لقیط کو اٹھانے والا (ملتقط ) اس کی پرورش میں جو کچھ بھی اس کی ذات پر کرے گا وہ احسان کے طور پر ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ اس لقط ( بچہ ) پر اس پانے والے (ملقط کو ) کو کچھ حکومت اور ولایت نہیں ہے۔ لیکن اگر قاضی اس پر خرچ کرنے کے لئے اس ملتقط کو ( اختیار بلکہ ) حکم دے دے کہ تم اس پر خرچ کرتے رہو تا کہ تمہارا خرچ اس شخص پر قرض رہ جائے (یعنی اس کے بالغ ہونے کے بعد اس سے وصول کر لینا۔ تو ایسا ہی کرنا ہو گا ) کیونکہ قاضی کی ولایت ہر شخص پر عام ہے ۔ (اور اگر قاضی نے اس سے یہ بات نہیں کہی ہو کہ تم جو کچھ بھی خرچ کرو گے اس لقیط پر قرض رہے گا تو صرف پرورش کا حکم دینے سے اس لقیط پر قرض باقی نہیں رہے گا۔ یہی قول اصبح ہے۔)
The expense of the foundling is covered from the treasury (Bait al-Mal). The person who picks up the foundling (multaqit), whatever he spends on his upbringing from his own self, will be as a favor. Because the finder has no authority or guardianship over the foundling. But if the judge gives him an order to spend, saying: 'Continue spending on him so that your expense becomes a loan upon this person (to be recovered after he becomes an adult), then it will be so. Because the judge's guardianship is general over everyone. (And if the judge did not tell him that whatever you spend will be a loan on the foundling, then merely ordering the upbringing does not make it a loan. This is the more correct opinion (asahh).)
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage handles the financial aspect of caring for a foundling (laqit). The ruling states that the primary responsibility for the foundling's expenses lies with the public treasury (Bait al-Mal). The finder (multaqit) who spends on the child does so voluntarily, as a favor (ehsan), because he has no legal authority over the child. However, if the judge (qadi) explicitly orders the finder to spend and states that it will be treated as a loan (qarz) recoverable from the foundling upon adulthood, then the spent amount becomes a debt. Without such explicit declaration by the judge, the mere order to provide upbringing does not create a loan. This is the relied-upon position (asahh).

Key Terms
لقیط (laqit): a foundling, an abandoned child found in the streets
ملتقط (multaqit): the person who picks up and takes custody of the foundling
بیت المال (Bait al-Mal): the public treasury of the Islamic state
قرض (qarz): loan; debt to be repaid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the finder's spending on a foundling become a loan (qarz) recoverable from the foundling?
A) Whenever the finder spends money.
B) Only if the judge explicitly orders him to spend and declares it as a loan.
C) Automatically upon the foundling reaching adulthood.
D) If the foundling agrees.

Key Takeaway
The finder's expenses on a foundling become a recoverable loan only if the judge explicitly orders it as such; otherwise it is a favor.

Priority of the Finder in Custody of a Foundling
Rule that no one can take a foundling from its finder due to prior possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a finder's prior possession (sabaq al-yad) establishes an exclusive right of custody over a foundling, and identify that it is a custodial right, not ownership.

Core Idea
Prior possession (sabaq al-yad) confers exclusive custodial right — not ownership.

The Hanafi school protects the finder of a foundling (laqit) by recognizing his prior physical act as the basis for a right of custody. This passage from the matn establishes that no one can take the foundling from the finder. However, this right is limited; it can be reviewed by a judge in the best interest of the child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is picking up a foundling (iltiqat). The effect is a right of custody (haqq al-hifz) established for the finder.
2
Analyze the basis: sabaq al-yad
The basis is prior physical possession. This is a recognized cause of right in Hanafi law.
Sabaq al-yad is also used in other areas like lost property (luqatah).
3
Distinguish between right of custody and ownership
The finder does not own the foundling; he has a custodial right that can be overridden by the judge.
Ownership (milk) is not established; only protection and care are required.
4
Consider implications: exclusivity vs. overriding authority
Exclusivity means no other individual can take the child, but the judge retains authority to reassign custody if necessary.
The judge acts as the guardian of the child's best interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ فَإِنِ الْتَقَطَهُ رَجُلٌ لَمْ يَكُنْ لِغَيْرِهِ أَن يَأْخُذَهُ مِنْهُ لأَنَّهُ تَبْتَ حَقُّ الْحِفْظِ لَهُ لِسَبَقِ يَدِهِ ترجمہ پھر اگر اس بچہ کو کوئی اٹھا کر لے آیا تو کسی دوسرے کو یہ اختیار نہیں ہو گا کہ اس ملتقط سے چھین لے یا مانگ لے کیونکہ پہلے لینے والے کو حفاظت کا حق حاصل ہو گیا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ اس کا ہاتھ اس پر پہلے پہنچا ہے۔
He (the author) said: So if a man takes custody of him (the foundling), no one else may take him from him, because the right of custody is established for him due to his prior hand. Translation: Then if someone picks up this child and brings him, no other person will have the authority to snatch or demand him from the finder, because the first taker has acquired the right of custody, since his hand reached him first.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn stating that once a person picks up a foundling, he obtains an exclusive right of custody (haqq al-hifz). No other person can forcibly take the child or demand him from the finder. The basis is the principle of 'sabaq al-yad' (precedence of hand). In Hanafi law, prior possession establishes a right that is protected against others. However, this right is not absolute ownership; it is a custodial right subject to other rules, such as the judge's authority to decide custody in the child's best interest.

Key Terms
سبق الید (sabaq al-yad): precedence of hand; prior physical possession

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the finder's exclusive right to custody of a foundling?
A) The finder's social status.
B) Prior physical possession (sabaq al-yad).
C) A written permission from the judge.
D) The consent of the foundling.

Key Takeaway
Prior possession (sabaq al-yad) secures for the finder of a foundling an exclusive custodial right, protected against seizure by others.

Luqatah — Found Property

Claim of Lineage by a Third Party or the Finder in Luqatah
Ruling on accepting a claimant's word for lineage via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on accepting a lineage claim for a foundling via istihsan and distinguish between the two qawls regarding its effect on the finder's custody.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas to establish a foundling's lineage, but its effect on custody remains disputed within the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in the chapter of luqatah (foundlings). When a person claims that a foundling is his child, should the claim be accepted and what happens to the finder's right of custody? The Hanafi school, by way of istihsan, prioritizes the child's benefit of lineage over the strict analogy (qiyas) that would reject the claim. However, within the school there are two opinions on whether the finder's custody is also nullified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the claim
The passage begins with a third party claiming that a foundling is his son. Note that the finder has not claimed lineage. Understand that the issue is about acceptance of the claim and its effects.
The finder's right of custody (yad al-multaqit) is at stake.
2
Understand the conflict between qiyas and istihsan
By qiyas, the claim would be rejected because it nullifies the finder's right. By istihsan, the claim is accepted because it benefits the child by giving him lineage. Identify the reasoning behind each.
Istihsan is a stronger principle in Hanafi fiqh for this case.
3
Trace the two qawls regarding effect on custody
After accepting the claim, some jurists say custody remains with the finder, others say it is invalidated. Note the practical difference: whether the child stays or goes.
The text does not name the jurists; these are internal Hanafi positions.
4
Examine the finder's own claim and the asahh view
When the finder himself claims lineage, some say it is valid by both qiyas and istihsan, but the asahh (most correct) view is that it is valid only by istihsan, not by qiyas.
The finder's claim is more problematic because it contradicts his earlier role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِن ادَّعَى مُدَعِيٌّ إِنَّهُ ابْنَهُ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ مَعْنَاهُ إِذَالَمُ يَدَّعِ الْمُلْتَقِطْ نَسَبَهُ وَهَذَا اسْتِحْسَانُ وَالْقِيَاسُ أَنْ لا يُقْبَلَ قَوْلُهُ لأنَّهُ يَتَضَمَّنُ إِبْطَالَ حَقَّ الْمُلْتَقِطِ وَجْهُ الْإِسْتِحْسَانِ أَنَّهُ إقْرَارٌ لِلصَّبِي بِمَا يَنفَعُهُ لأنه يَتَشَرَّف بالنسب ويُعير بعَدَمِه ثُمَّ قِيلَ يَصِحُ فِى حَقَّهِ دُونَ إِبْطَال يَدِ الْمُلْتَقِطِ وَقِيلَ يَبْتَنِي عَلَيْهِ بُطْلانُ يَدِهِ وَلَوْادَّعَاهُ الْمُلْتَقِطْ قِيْلَ يَصِحُ قِيَاسًا وَ اسْتِحْسَانًا وَالْاصَحُ أَنَّهُ عَلَى الْقِيَاسِ وَ الْاِسْتِحْسَانِ وَقَدْعُرِفَ فِي الْأَصْلِ. ترجمہ اس کے بعد اگر کسی نے یہ دعوی کیا کہ یہ میرا بیٹا ہے تو اسی کی بات مقبول ہوگی ۔ اس مسئلہ کی صورت یہ ہوگی کہ پانے والے نے اس بچہ پر اپنے نسب کا دعوای کبھی نہیں کیا) بلکہ صرف پانے کا دعوی کیا ہے اور اس دوسرے نے تو اس پر اپنے نسب کا دعوی کیا ہے ۔ اور ایسا نسب صرف دعوی سے بھی ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔ یہ حکم استحسان کے ساتھ ہے۔ کیونکہ قیاس کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ اس کا صرف دعوای نسب مقبول نہ ہو۔ کیونکہ وہ یہ دعوی کر کے اس پانے والے کی حفاظت کے حق کو ختم کر دینا چاہتا ہے۔ اور استحسان کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس مدعی نسب کا قول اس بچہ کے حق میں ہونے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اس کی ذات سے بچہ کو فائدہ حاصل ہو جائے ۔ کیونکہ اس بچہ کا نسب ثابت ہونے سے اس کی بڑائی اور بزرگی بڑھتی ہے اور یہ نہ ہونے سے وہ ہمیشہ کے لئے ثبوت نسب سے محروم ہونے کے احساس سے شرمندہ ہوتا رہے گا۔ پھر ایک قول یہ ہے کہ مدعی کا دعوای صرف اس سے نسب ہونے میں صحیح ہو گا۔ لیکن اس سے اٹھا کر لانے والے کا قبضہ ختم نہیں ہوگا ( کیونکہ وہ اس کے پاس رہے گا۔ ) اور دوسرا قول یہ ہے کہ نسب ثابت ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اس پانے والے کا قبضہ بھی اس سے باطل ہو جائے گا ( بچہ اس کے پاس سے چلا جائے گا ) اور اگر پانے والے نے خود بھی اس بچہ پر نسب کا دعوی کر دیا تو کہا گیا ہے کہ قیاس اور استحسان دونوں اعتبار سے اس کا کہنا صحیح ہوگا۔ لیکن اصح قول یہ ہے کہ اس صورت میں بھی قیاس میں صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ اگر چہ استحسان سے صحیح ہوگا چنانچہ
If a claimant claims that he is his son, then his word is accepted. Meaning, when the finder has not claimed his lineage. This is istihsan. The qiyas is that his word should not be accepted because it entails nullifying the finder's right. The face of istihsan is that it is an acknowledgment for the child of what benefits him, because he is honored by lineage and disgraced by its absence. Then it is said: it is valid with respect to his right without nullifying the finder's custody. And it is said: it (the claim) entails the nullification of his custody. And if the finder himself claims him, it is said it is valid by qiyas and istihsan. The most correct (asahh) is that it is according to qiyas and istihsan, and it is known in the original. Translation: After that, if someone claims that this is my son, then his statement will be accepted. The scenario of this issue is that the finder has never claimed lineage of this child, but only claimed finding; and this other person has claimed lineage. And such lineage is established merely by claim. This ruling is by istihsan. Because the requirement of qiyas is that his claim of lineage should not be accepted, because by making this claim he wants to terminate the finder's right of custody. The reason for istihsan is that the statement of this claimant of lineage is for the benefit of the child, meaning from his self, the child gains benefit, because by establishing the child's lineage, his honor and dignity increase, and without it he would forever feel ashamed due to deprivation of lineage. Then one opinion is that the claimant's claim of lineage is valid only for establishing the relationship, but the finder's custody is not terminated (the child will remain with him). Another opinion is that due to establishment of lineage, the finder's custody becomes invalid (the child will leave him). And if the finder himself also claims lineage of this child, then it is said that by both qiyas and istihsan his statement is correct. But the most correct (asahh) opinion is that in this case too, by qiyas it is not correct, although by istihsan it is correct, as such...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where someone other than the finder claims that the foundling is his son. The Hanafi position is that such a claim is accepted by way of istihsan (juristic preference) to benefit the child, even though qiyas would reject it because it would strip the finder of his right. The text then records two opinions within the school regarding the effect of such a claim: (1) the claim establishes lineage but does not remove the finder's custody, and (2) it removes the finder's custody. The passage also addresses the finder's own claim of lineage: some say it is accepted by both qiyas and istihsan, but the most correct view (asahh) is that it is valid only by istihsan, not by qiyas (since qiyas would conflict with his earlier position as a mere finder). The term 'al-asl' likely refers to the foundational work (al-Asl or al-Mabsut).

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): juristic preference; a method of reasoning that gives preference to a more equitable result over strict analogy
قیاس (qiyas): analogical reasoning
قول (qawl): opinion; statement of a jurist

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct (asahh) view, is the finder's own claim of lineage accepted by qiyas?
A) Yes, by both qiyas and istihsan.
B) No, it is not accepted by qiyas but is accepted by istihsan.
C) It is not accepted at all.
D) It is accepted only if the judge orders it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi luqatah, a lineage claim for a foundling is accepted by istihsan for the child's benefit, but its effect on the finder's custody is subject to two qawls.

Luqatah — Found Property
beginner
6 min
Priority Among Two Claimants of a Foundling's Lineage
Rule of priority among two lineage claimants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rules of priority when two individuals claim a foundling's lineage and explain why bayyinah overrides a description of a mark.

Core Idea
Describing a unique mark gives priority; if none, both are equal; first claim wins unless the other brings solid evidence.

This passage from Al-Hidayah deals with a rare situation: two people both claim that a foundling is their child. The jurists provide a hierarchy of proof: first, a physical mark, then temporal priority, and finally formal evidence (bayyinah). Understanding these rules clarifies how Hanafi law resolves competing claims when direct proof is lacking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
There is a foundling (luqatah) and two men claim him as their son. No prior father is known. The question is which claimant is entitled to the child's lineage.
2
Apply the first scenario: a mark is described
One claimant correctly describes a unique physical mark on the child. The text says he is more entitled (awla) because the apparent (zahir) supports him due to the mark matching his claim.
The mark must be an accurate description; if both describe marks, further analysis may be needed.
3
Apply the second scenario: no mark described
Neither claimant describes a mark. The child becomes the son of both (ibnuhuma). This is a special ruling based on equality of cause (istawa' fi al-sabab).
This does not mean literal dual paternity but a legal attribution for the sake of custody and maintenance.
4
Apply the third scenario: priority by earlier claim
If one claims first without describing a mark, he is preferred, because his right was established at a time when there was no opponent. However, this is overridden if the other brings bayyinah (clear evidence).
Bayyinah here means testimony of witnesses.
5
Compare the strength of proofs
Bayyinah (evidence) is stronger than a mark (alamah). Even if one described a mark or claimed first, bayyinah from the other claimant would make him more entitled. The hierarchy: bayyinah > temporal priority > description of mark.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِن ادَّعَاهُ إِنْسَانٌ وَوَصَفَ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَامَةً فِى جَسَدِهِ فَهُوَ أَوْلَى بِهِ لأنَّ الظَّاهِرَ شَاهِدٌ لَهُ لِمُوَافَقَةِ الْعَلَامَةِ كَلَامِهِ وإن لم يصف أحَدُهُمَا عَلَامَةً فَهُوَإبْنُهُمَا لِإِسْتَوَائِهِمَا فِى السَّبَب وَلَوْ سَبَقَتْ دَعْوَةَ أحَدِهِمَا فَهُوَابْتُهُ لأنَّهُ ثَبَتَ حَقَّهُ فِي زَمَانِ لَا مُنَازِعَ لَهُ فِيهِ إِلَّا إِذَا أَقَامَ الْأَخَرُ الْبَيِّنَةَ لَانَّ الْبَيِّنَةَ أَقْوَى ترجمہ اور اگر اس پانے والے کے سوا دو آدمیوں نے اس پر نسب کا دعوی کر دیا لیکن ان میں سے ایک نے اس پائے ہوئے بچہ کے بدن میں کوئی خاص علامت بتائی تو وہی زیادہ حقدار اور اولی ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ بظاہر گواہ اسی کے ساتھ ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ علامت اس کے دعوی کے مطابق ہے۔ اور اگر کسی نے اس کی کوئی خاص علامت نہیں بتائی تو یہ بچہ ان دونوں کا لڑکا کہلائے گا کیونکہ دعوی کرنے میں دونوں برابر کے ہیں ۔ اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک نے پہلے دعوی کر دیا تب بچہ اسی کی طرف منسوب ہوگا کیونکہ اس کا حق ایسے وقت میں ثابت ہوا جبکہ اس کا کوئی مدمقابل نہیں تھا۔ لیکن اگر دوسرے نے اس کے ساتھ اپنا مستقل گواہ بھی پیش کر دیا تو یہی مقدم اور زیادہ حقدار ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ علامات کے مقابلہ میں گواہی زیادہ قوی دلیل ہوتی ہے۔
And if two men claim him, and one of them describes a mark on his body, then he is more entitled to him, because the apparent (evidence) is a witness for him due to the mark matching his statement. If neither describes a mark, then he is the son of both, due to equality in the cause. And if one of them made the claim earlier, then he is his son, because his right was established at a time when there was no opponent for him, unless the other brings clear evidence (bayyinah), because evidence is stronger. Translation: And if, besides the finder, two men claim lineage on him, but one of them describes a specific mark on the body of the foundling child, then that one is more entitled and prior. Because apparently the witness is with him, as this mark matches his claim. And if neither describes a specific mark, then this child will be called the son of both, because in making the claim they are equal. And if one of them made the claim earlier, then the child will be attributed to him, because his right was established at a time when he had no opponent. But if the other brings his own independent evidence (bayyinah), then this one becomes preferred and more entitled, because evidence is a stronger proof than marks.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage provides the rules for when two separate individuals claim that a foundling is their son. The primary determinant is the description of a unique physical mark (alamah) on the child's body: the one who correctly describes it is preferred. If neither describes a mark, then both are considered fathers (an unusual ruling that seems to imply both claims are accepted equally). If one claims first, he is preferred, unless the other brings a clear evidence (bayyinah, such as witnesses). This prioritization is based on the principle that evidence (bayyinah) is stronger than a physical descriptor (alamah). Note that this is a specific scenario within the Hanafi law of foundlings (al-luqatah).

Key Terms
علامة (alamah): a distinguishing physical mark on the body
بينة (bayyinah): clear evidence, usually testimony of witnesses

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The age of the claimant.
B) The wealth of the claimant.
C) Clear evidence (bayyinah) presented by one claimant.
D) The finder's preference.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when two claimants seek a foundling's lineage, priority is given first to the one who describes a physical mark, then to the one who claims first, but formal evidence (bayyinah) overrides all.

Foundling and Dhimmi Claim: Lineage Accepted, Islam Preserved
Ruling with proof using istihsan and tab'id

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dhimmi's paternity claim over a foundling and explain how istihsan splits the claim into accepted and rejected parts based on benefit and harm.

Core Idea
When a dhimmi claims a foundling, lineage is established but the child remains Muslim — benefit and harm are separated.

In this passage, al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced case: a foundling discovered in Muslim territory is presumed Muslim. When a dhimmi claims the child as his son, two conflicting outcomes arise — establishing lineage (beneficial) vs. changing the child's religion (harmful). The Hanafi school applies istihsan to accept the beneficial part and reject the harmful, demonstrating how legal reasoning prioritizes the child's welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that a foundling is found in Muslim territory, and a dhimmi (non-Muslim citizen) claims the child is his son. Note the default ruling: the foundling is Muslim because of the location.
2
Decompose the claim into two parts
The dhimmi's claim implicitly includes: (1) establishment of lineage (nasab), which gives the child known parentage and rights; (2) nullification of the child's Islam, making him a dhimmi like the father.
3
Evaluate each part for benefit and harm
Lineage is beneficial for the child (identity, inheritance, support). Changing his religion is harmful because Islam was already established by the territory and cannot be undone without evidence.
The child's Islam is thabit (established) by dar al-Islam.
4
Apply istihsan to split the claim
Qiyas would accept the entire claim and make the child a dhimmi. Istihsan overrides this to accept only the beneficial part, rejecting the harmful part. This is called tab'id (splitting).
Istihsan is a stronger proof here due to the child's interest.
5
State the final ruling
Lineage from the dhimmi is established (sahih), but the child remains Muslim. The claim is effective only insofar as it benefits the child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ إِذَا وُجِدَ فِى مِصْرِمِنْ آمُصَارِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ اَوْفِى قَرْيَةٍ مِنْ قُرَاهُمُ فَادَّعَى ذِمّى أَنَّهُ إِبْتُهُ ثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُ مِنْهُ وَكَانَ مُسْلِمًا وَهذَا اسْتِحْسَانُ لِأَنَّ دَعْوَاهُ تَضَمَّنَ النَّسَبَ وَهُوَنَا فِعٌ لِلصَّغِيرِ وَابْطَالُ الْإِسْلَامِ الثَّابِتِ بِالدَّارِ وَهُوَ يَضُرُّهُ فَصَحَّتْ دَعْوَتُهُ فِيْمَا يَنفَعُهُ دُونَ مَا يَضُرُّهُ. ترجمہ اور اگر پڑا ہوا بچہ مسلمانوں کے شہروں میں سے کسی شہر میں یا دیہاتوں میں سے کسی دیہات میں ملا۔ اور کسی ڈمی نے اس کے بارے میں یہ دعوی کیا کہ وہ میرا بیٹا ہے تو اس کا نسب اس سے ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ البتہ اس بچہ کو مسلمان مانا جائے گا۔ اور یہ حکم استحسان کی بناء پر ہے۔ کیونکہ اس ذمی کے دعوی میں دو باتیں ہیں ایک نسب کا ثبوت تو یہ بات اس بچہ کے حق میں مفید ہے۔ دوسری بات اس بچہ کے اسلام کو ختم کر کے ذمی ثابت کرنا ۔ حالانکہ دارالاسلام میں اس بچہ کے پائے جانے سے اس کا حق اسلام ثابت ہے جس کو ختم کرنا اس بچہ کے حق میں نقصان دہ ہے۔ لہذا پہلا دعوی یعنی ثبوت نسب کا تو وہ صیح مانا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اسی میں بچہ کا فائدہ ہے ۔ اور اسلام مٹانے کے بارے میں صحیح نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ یہ بچہ کے حق میں نقصان دہ ہے۔
And if he is found in a city from among the cities of the Muslims, or in a village from among their villages, and a dhimmi claims that he is his son, his lineage from him is established, and he (the child) remains Muslim. This is istihsan, because his claim entails lineage, which is beneficial for the child, and nullification of the Islam established by (being found in) the abode (of Islam), which harms him. So his claim is accepted in what benefits him and not in what harms him. Translation: And if the foundling child is found in a city among the Muslim cities or in a village among their villages, and some dhimmi makes a claim about him that he is his son, then his lineage from him will be established, but the child will be considered Muslim. This ruling is based on istihsan. Because the dhimmi's claim involves two things: first, establishment of lineage – this is beneficial for the child. Second, nullification of the child's Islam and establishing him as a dhimmi – while the child's Islam is established by being found in the abode of Islam, and nullifying it is harmful to the child. Therefore, the first claim – establishment of lineage – is accepted because the child benefits from it, but regarding nullification of Islam, it is not valid because it is harmful to the child.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
When a foundling is found in Muslim territory (dar al-Islam), the default ruling is that the child is Muslim. If a dhimmi (non-Muslim citizen of the Islamic state) claims the child as his son, the lineage is established by istihsan because it is beneficial for the child to have known parentage. However, the child's Islamic status cannot be changed by this claim, because the child's Islam was established by the location of his discovery, and changing it would harm the child. Therefore, the claim is split (tab'id): the beneficial part (lineage) is accepted, but the harmful part (changing religion) is rejected. This is a clear example of istihsan overriding qiyas, as qiyas would have accepted the entire claim and made the child a dhimmi.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): a non-Muslim living under Muslim rule with a covenant of protection
تابيد (tab'id): splitting the claim into accepted and rejected parts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a foundling is found in Muslim land and a dhimmi claims him, what is established?
A) The child becomes a dhimmi.
B) Only the lineage from the dhimmi is established, and the child remains Muslim.
C) Both lineage and religion are changed to dhimmi.
D) The claim is rejected entirely.

Key Takeaway
Lineage from a dhimmi claimant is established for a foundling in Muslim land, but the child's Islamic status remains unchanged by virtue of being found in Dar al-Islam.

Foundling in Dhimmi Territory: Location, Finder, and Transmission Variance
Ruling on foundling in dhimmi territory with transmission variance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling on a foundling's religion when found in dhimmi territory and identify when the ruling is definitive versus when variant transmissions exist.

Core Idea
A foundling's religion follows the location, but only definitively when the finder shares that religion.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the religious status of a foundling discovered in dhimmi territory or places of worship. The passage from Al-Hidayah presents a clear default rule but introduces an important nuance: the ruling is definitive only when the finder is also a dhimmi. Otherwise, the school records variant transmissions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the law to diverse scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
Read the passage and note the two main scenarios: (1) foundling found in a dhimmi village or religious building, and (2) the same but with a Muslim finder or a dhimmi finder in Muslim territory.
The location is the primary factor, but the finder's religion introduces complexity.
2
Note the default ruling
When both location and finder are dhimmi, the ruling is definitive: the child is a dhimmi. This is supported by a single transmission (riwayah wahidah).
A single transmission indicates a settled, reliable ruling within the school.
3
Recognize the variant transmissions
When the finder's religion differs from the location (Muslim finder in dhimmi place, or dhimmi finder in Muslim place), the transmissions differ (ikhtalafat al-riwayah). This means no single ruling is established.
Variant transmissions often lead to later scholars choosing one based on other principles.
4
Apply the framework
Given a specific scenario, determine whether the ruling is definitive or subject to ikhtilaf. If definitive, apply the default. If variant, a mufti must consider the strengths of the transmissions or refer to later tarjih.
This skill is essential for issuing fatwas in this area.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ وُجِدَفِي قَرْيَةٍ مِنْ قُرى أهل الدِّمَةِ أوْفِى بِيعَةٍ أَوْ كَنِيسَةٍ كَانَ ذِمِيًّا وَهَذَا الْجَوَابُ فِيْمَا إِذَا كَانَ الْوَاحِدُ ذِ مِيَّا رِوَايَةً وَاحِدَةً وَإِنْ كَانَ الْوَاجِدُ مُسْلِمًا فِى هذا الْمَكَان اَوْ ذِمَيَّافِى مكان الْمُسْلِمِينَ اخْتَلَفَتِ الرّوَايَةُ ترجمہ اور اگر پڑا ہوا بچہ ڈمیوں کی بستیوں میں سے کسی بستی میں یا کسی گرجے یا عبادت گاہ میں ملے تو وہ ڈمی ہوگا۔ اور یہ فیصلہ اسی صورت میں ہے کہ پانے والا بھی ڈمی ہو۔ اس بارے میں ایک ہی روایت ہے۔ اور اگر پانے والا مسلمان ہو اس جگہ میں (جہاں بچہ ملا ہے) یا ڈمی ہو مسلمانوں کی جگہ میں تو اس بارے میں مختلف روایتیں ہیں۔
And if he is found in a village from among the villages of the people of dhimma, or in a church or synagogue, he is a dhimmi. This answer is when the finder is a dhimmi – that is one transmission. And if the finder is a Muslim in this place, or a dhimmi in a Muslim place, then the transmission differs. Translation: And if the foundling child is found in a village among the villages of the dhimmis or in a church or monastery, then he will be a dhimmi. And this ruling applies only when the finder is also a dhimmi – there is only one transmission on this. And if the finder is a Muslim in this place (where the child was found) or a dhimmi in a Muslim place, then the transmissions differ.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage deals with the religious status of a foundling found in non-Muslim territory or places of worship. The default ruling is that the child takes the religion of the location: if found in dhimmi villages or their religious buildings (church, synagogue), the child is considered dhimmi. However, this ruling is only definitive when the finder (multaqit) is also a dhimmi. If the finder is a Muslim in such a place, or a dhimmi finds the child in a Muslim area, then there are variant transmissions (ikhtilaf al-riwayah) within the Hanafi school, meaning the ruling is not settled. This illustrates that the school recorded different narrations from the founding imams on certain subsidiary issues.

Key Terms
رواية (riwayah): transmission; a version of a ruling narrated from an imam
اختلفت الرواية (ikhtalafat al-riwayah): the transmissions differ; indicating a lack of consensus within the school

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the child is found in a dhimmi village and the finder is a dhimmi.
C) When the child is found in a Muslim city and the finder is a dhimmi.
D) When the child is found in a dhimmi village and the finder is Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The foundling's religion follows the location, but the ruling is definitive only when the finder shares that religion; otherwise, the school has variant transmissions.

Prioritizing Claims to a Foundling's Lineage: Describing a Mark, Precedence, and Evidence
Ruling on two claimants for a foundling's lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for resolving competing claims to a foundling's lineage based on describing a mark, precedence of claim, and formal evidence, and apply these rules to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A mark described on the foundling's body gives preference to the claimant; but formal evidence (bayyinah) overrides all other considerations.

When two individuals claim a foundling as their child, how does Hanafi law resolve the conflict? This passage from Al-Hidayah outlines three criteria: describing a concealed mark, order of claim, and formal evidence. Understanding these rules is essential for cases of missing lineage where proof is scarce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three situations presented: (a) one describes a mark, (b) neither describes a mark, (c) one claims earlier but mark absent. Then state the ruling for each.
2
Extract the dalil for each scenario
For each scenario, identify the proof: (a) the apparent evidence (zahir) supports the one with the mark, (b) equality in cause leads to shared paternity, (c) earlier claim establishes right unless bayyinah overrides.
3
Compare the strength of proofs
Note that describing a mark is weaker than formal evidence (bayyinah). The passage explicitly says 'bayyinah is stronger', so precedence of claim is overridden by bayyinah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ادَّعَاهُ إِنْسَانٌ وَوَصَفَ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَامَةً فِى جَسَدِهِ فَهُوَ أَوْلَى بِهِ لأنَّ الظَّاهِرَ شَاهِدٌ لَهُ لِمُوَافَقَةِ الْعَلَامَةِ كَلَامِهِ وإن لم يصف أحَدُهُمَا عَلَامَةً فَهُوَإبْنُهُمَا لِإِسْتَوَائِهِمَا فِى السَّبَب وَلَوْ سَبَقَتْ دَعْوَةَ أحَدِهِمَا فَهُوَابْتُهُ لأنَّهُ ثَبَتَ حَقَّهُ فِي زَمَانِ لَا مُنَازِعَ لَهُ فِيهِ إِلَّا إِذَا أَقَامَ الْأَخَرُ الْبَيِّنَةَ لَانَّ الْبَيَّنَةَ أَقْوَى
And if two persons claim him, and one of them describes a mark on his body, then he is more entitled to him because the apparent evidence testifies for him due to the mark matching his claim. And if neither describes a mark, then he is the son of both because of their equality in cause. And if one of them made the claim earlier, then he is his son because his right was established at a time when there was no contestant, unless the other brings evidence, for evidence is stronger.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a foundling claimed by two individuals, based on the text of Al-Hidayah. The commentary expands on three scenarios. First, if one claimant describes a concealed mark on the child's body, that claimant is preferred because the mark corroborates his claim, making the apparent evidence (zahir) support him. Second, if neither describes a mark, the child is attributed to both, meaning he inherits from both and both are liable for his maintenance, due to equal claim strength. Third, if one claims before the other without a mark, the prior claimant becomes the sole father, as his right was established without contest. However, this priority is overridden if the other claimant later brings formal evidence (bayyinah), because evidence is stronger than mere precedence. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that in lineage disputes, apparent signs and order of claim matter, but definitive proof (bayyinah) always prevails.

Key Terms
لقيط (laqīṭ): A foundling; a child found abandoned whose lineage and status are unknown.
علامة (ʿalāmah): A distinctive mark on the body that serves as evidence for the claim.
بينة (bayyinah): Formal legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child is divided between them equally.
B) The one who describes the mark is preferred and the child is his.
C) The first claimant gets the child regardless of the mark.
D) The case is referred to the qadi for ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when two claimants seek a foundling's lineage, describing a concealed mark gives priority, but formal evidence (bayyinah) always overrides.

Dhimmi Claim to Foundling: Split by Benefit and Harm
Ruling on dhimmi's claim to foundling in Muslim territory with istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dhimmi's claim to a foundling in Dar al-Islam and explain the istihsan reasoning that splits the claim.

Core Idea
When a dhimmi claims paternity of a foundling, lineage is established but Islam is preserved — benefit accepted, harm rejected.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced case: a foundling (laqit) discovered in a Muslim settlement is claimed by a dhimmi. The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to accept the beneficial part of the claim (lineage) while rejecting the harmful part (changing the child's religion). This demonstrates a key principle: legal claims are split when they combine benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two components
The foundling is found in a Muslim city or village (Dar al-Islam). The dhimmi's claim has two parts: establishing lineage (beneficial) and making the child a dhimmi (harmful if the child is Muslim).
2
Apply the default presumption
By default, a foundling in Dar al-Islam is presumed Muslim because the abode establishes Islam. This is a well-known Hanafi principle.
This presumption is qat'i (definitive) for the child's religion.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The qiyas would be to accept or reject the claim as a whole. But istihsan splits it: the claim includes both benefit (lineage) and harm (nullifying Islam). The beneficial part is accepted; the harmful part is rejected.
Istihsan here is based on the principle of choosing the stronger benefit and avoiding harm.
4
Derive the ruling
The dhimmi's claim establishes the child's lineage (the child becomes his son for inheritance, custody, etc.), but the child remains Muslim. The two effects are separated.
5
Note the authority and cross-madhhab status
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position. Other schools may consider the entire claim or reject it, but Hanafi separates benefit from harm.
The text says 'this is istihsan', indicating the school's reliance on istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ إِذَا وُجِدَ فِى مِصْرِمِنْ آمُصَارِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ اَوْفِى قَرْيَةٍ مِنْ قُرى الْمُسْلِمِينَ فَادَّعَى ذِمّى أَنَّهُ إِبْتُهُ ثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُ مِنْهُ وَكَانَ مُسْلِمًا وَهذَا اسْتِحْسَانُ لِأَنَّ دَعْوَاهُ تَضَمَّنَ النَّسَبَ وَهُوَ نَافِعٌ لِلصَّغِيرِ وَابْطَالُ الْإِسْلَامِ الثَّابِتِ بِالدَّارِ وَهُوَ يَضُرُّهُ فَصَحَّتْ دَعْوَتُهُ فِيْمَا يَنْفَعُهُ دُونَ مَا يَضُرُّهُ.
And if he is found in a city among the cities of the Muslims or in a village among their villages, and a dhimmi claims that he is his son, his lineage is established from him, and the child remains Muslim. This is istihsan, because his claim includes lineage (which benefits the child) and nullification of Islam established by the abode (which harms the child). So his claim is accepted in what benefits him and not in what harms him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on a foundling discovered in a Muslim settlement when a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) claims paternity. The ruling is derived by istihsan (juristic preference). The dhimmi's claim has two components: establishing lineage (beneficial to the child) and making the child a dhimmi (harmful). Since the child is found in Dar al-Islam, Islam is already established for him by virtue of the abode (as per the principle that a foundling in Muslim territory is presumed Muslim). Therefore, the claim is accepted for lineage only, not for changing the child's religion. This shows that in Hanafi fiqh, when a claim mixes benefit and harm, only the beneficial part is accepted. The authority weight: This is from Zahir al-Riwayah as it appears in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under the protection of an Islamic state.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from a qiyas ruling for a stronger evidence or necessity.
دار الإسلام (Dār al-Islām): Territory under Muslim rule where Islamic law is implemented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A foundling is found in a Muslim city. A dhimmi claims the child is his son. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The child becomes a dhimmi and his lineage is established.
B) The child remains Muslim, but his lineage is not established.
C) The child remains Muslim and his lineage is established.
D) The claim is rejected entirely because the child is presumed free.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a dhimmi's claim to a foundling establishes lineage but does not change the child's religion, because the beneficial part is accepted and the harmful part is rejected by istihsan.

Foundling in Dhimmi Settlement: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Place vs Finder
Ruling and narration differences on foundling in dhimmi locale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi narrations on determining a foundling's religion when the finder's religion differs from the location, and explain the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
A foundling's religion can be determined by place, finder, or benefit — three valid Hanafi narrations.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute: when a foundling is found in a dhimmi locale but the finder is Muslim (or vice versa), which should govern the child's religious attribution? The school transmitted multiple narrations, each with its own legal reasoning. Understanding these narrations refines your grasp of how Hanafi jurists weigh different factors like possession (yad), precedence (sabq), and the child's welfare.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling
When a foundling is found in a dhimmi village, church, or synagogue, and the finder is also a dhimmi, the child is a dhimmi by unanimous narration. This is the starting point before the dispute arises.
The base ruling applies only when finder and place agree.
2
Recognize the Trigger for Dispute
The dispute arises when the finder's religion differs from the place: a Muslim finder in a dhimmi area, or a dhimmi finder in a Muslim area. In these cases, the narrations diverge.
If the finder's religion matches the place, no dispute.
3
State the First Narration (Kitab al-Laqit): Place
In the narration of Kitab al-Laqit, the place is considered (child follows the predominant religion of the location) because of its precedence (sabq). Thus, a foundling in a dhimmi area is dhimmi even if the finder is Muslim.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
State the Second Narration (Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad): Finder
In the narration of Kitab al-Da'wa via Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, the finder is considered due to the strength of possession (quwwat al-yad). The analogy: if a child is captured with one parent, the child follows that parent's religion, demonstrating that personal attachment outweighs the abode.
So a Muslim finder makes the child Muslim even in a dhimmi locale.
5
State the Third Narration (Some Copies): Benefit of Child
In some copies, the child's benefit is considered: Islam is chosen because it is better for the child's welfare. This prioritizes the child's long-term spiritual and worldly interest.
This narration is mentioned but not attributed to a specific source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ وُجِدَ فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِنْ قُرى أَهْلِ الذِّمَّةِ أَوْفِى بِيْعَةٍ أَوْ كَنِيسَةٍ كَانَ ذِمِيًّا وَهَذَا الْجَوَابُ فِيْمَا إِذَا كَانَ الْوَاحِدُ ذِمِيَّا رِوَايَةً وَاحِدَةً وَإِنْ كَانَ الْوَاجِدُ مُسْلِمًا فِى هذا الْمَكَان اَوْ ذِمَيَّا فِى مَكَانِ الْمُسْلِمِينَ اخْتَلَفَتِ الرّوَايَةُ فِيهِ فَفِي رِوَايَةِ كِتَابُ اللَّقِيْطِ اعْتُبِرَ الْمَكَانَ لِسَبْقِهِ وَفِي كِتَابِ الدَّعْوَى فِي بَعْضِ التَّسْخِ اعْتُبِرَ الْوَاجِدُ وَهُوَ رِوَايَةٌ ابْنُ سَمَاعَة عَنْ مُحَمَّدٍ لِقُوَّةِ الْيَدِ الا ترى إِنَّ تَبْعِيَّةَ لأبَوَيْنِ فَوْقَ تَبْعِيَّةِ الدَّارِ حَتَّى إِذَا سُبِيَ مَعَ الصَّغِيرِ أَحَدُهُمَا يُعْتَبَرُ كَافِرًا وَ فِي بَعْضٍ نُسْخَةٍ اعْتُبِرَ الْإِسْلَامُ نَظَرًا لِلصَّغِيرِ
And if he is found in a village among the villages of the people of dhimma, or in a church, or a synagogue, he is a dhimmi. This is the answer when the finder is a dhimmi, in one narration. And if the finder is a Muslim in this place, or a dhimmi in a Muslim place, the narrations differ. In the narration of Kitab al-Laqit, the place is considered due to its precedence; and in Kitab al-Da'wa in some copies, the finder is considered, and this is the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad due to the strength of possession. Do you not see that following the parents is stronger than following the abode? Even when one of the parents is captured with the child, he is considered a disbeliever. And in some copies, Islam is considered for the benefit of the child.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a foundling in a dhimmi settlement when the finder is not a dhimmi. The base ruling: if a foundling is found in a dhimmi village, church, or synagogue, and the finder is also a dhimmi, the child is a dhimmi (unanimous). However, if the finder is a Muslim (in a dhimmi place) or a dhimmi (in a Muslim place), there are three narrations. First, from Kitab al-Laqit: consideration is given to the place (so if found in dhimmi area, child is dhimmi regardless of finder). Second, from Kitab al-Da'wa (via Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad): consideration is given to the finder (so if the finder is Muslim, child is Muslim, even in dhimmi locale), based on the strength of possession (quwwat al-yad). The analogy with parents: if a child is captured with one parent, the child follows that parent in religion, showing that personal attachment outweighs the abode. Third, in some copies, consideration is given to the child's benefit (so always Muslim as Islam is better for the child). The commentary notes these as different authoritative transmissions within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
رواية (riwāyah): A narration or transmission of a ruling from an earlier authority, often with a chain.
اليد (al-yad): Possession or control; here it refers to the finder's physical custody of the foundling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A foundling is found in a church in a dhimmi village by a Muslim. According to the narration of Kitab al-Da'wa (Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad), what is the child's religion?
A) The child is a dhimmi because of the place.
B) The child is Muslim because of the finder.
C) The child is a Muslim because of benefit.
D) The child's religion is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has multiple valid narrations on a foundling's religion when finder and place differ; a mufti must know all three.

Claiming Foundling as Slave and Slave's Claim of Paternity
Rulings on claims of slavery and a slave's paternity for a foundling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on claims of ownership and paternity regarding a foundling, and explain the underlying presumption of freedom and the principle that doubt does not remove certainty.

Core Idea
The foundling is presumptively free; a claim of slavery requires evidence, but a slave's paternity claim is accepted while the child remains free.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses two distinct scenarios involving a foundling (laqit): someone claiming the foundling is his slave, and a slave claiming the foundling is his son. The rulings demonstrate the strong Hanafi presumption of freedom (hurriyyah zahirah) and the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
The passage presents two separate claims regarding a foundling: an ownership claim ('he is my slave') and a paternity claim ('he is my son').
Each claim has a different legal outcome.
2
Analyze the ownership claim
The claim is rejected unless evidence is brought. The default status of the foundling is free (hurr zahiran). This presumption can only be overcome by clear proof (bayyinah).
This upholds the Islamic principle that freedom is the original state.
3
Analyze the paternity claim
A slave's claim to paternity is accepted because it benefits the child (establishing lineage) and does not harm anyone. The child remains free despite the father being a slave.
The child's freedom is protected by the presumption and the fact that a slave can have free children from a free woman.
4
Understand the reasoning behind paternity acceptance
Benefit to the child is a valid consideration (maslahah). Also, since a slave can marry a free woman, her child is free. The child's apparent freedom is not invalidated by doubt about the father's status.
The ruling applies the maxim: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt'.
5
Apply the key principles
Summarize: (1) Al-asl al-hurriyyah – freedom is default. (2) Al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk – certainty of freedom persists despite doubt. (3) Benefit (manfa'ah) can justify establishing lineage.
These principles are foundational in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنِ ادَّعى أن اللقيط عَبْدَهُ لَمْ يُقْبَلُ مِنْهُ لأنَّهُ حُرٌّ ظَاهِرًا إِلَّا أَنْ يُقِيمَ الْبَيِّنَةَ أَنَّهُ عَبْدُهُ. فَإِن ادَّعَى عَبْدٌ أَنَّهُ إِبْتُهُ ثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُ مِنْهُ لِأَنَّهُ يَنْفَعُهُ وَكَانَ حُرًّا لِأَنَّ الْمَمْلُوكَ قَدْ تَلِدُ لَهُ الْحُرَّةُ فَلَا تَبْطُلُ الْحُرِّيَّةُ الظَّاهِرِيَّةُ بِالشَّكِّ.
And whoever claims that the foundling is his slave, it is not accepted from him because he is apparently free, unless he brings evidence that he is his slave. And if a slave claims that he is his son, his lineage is established from him because it benefits him, and the child remains free, because a free woman may give birth to a child from a slave husband, so the apparent freedom is not invalidated by doubt.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage provides two rulings. First, if someone claims a foundling is his slave, the claim is rejected because the child is presumptively free (al-asl al-hurriyyah). The claimant must bring formal evidence (bayyinah) to overcome this presumption. This upholds the Islamic principle that freedom is the default status. Second, if a slave claims the foundling is his son, the claim is accepted for lineage, but the child remains free. The reasoning: the child benefits by having a known lineage, and since a slave can marry a free woman and have free children, the child's apparent freedom (due to being found in Dar al-Islam) is not negated by doubt. This demonstrates the Hanafi method of separating beneficial and harmful aspects of a claim, and the heavy presumption of freedom.

Key Terms
حرية ظاهرة (hurriyyah zāhirah): Apparent freedom; the legal presumption that a person is free until proven otherwise.
شك (shakk): Doubt; uncertainty that does not invalidate a legal presumption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a slave's claim that a foundling is his son?
A) The claim is rejected because the slave cannot own property.
B) The claim is accepted but the child becomes a slave.
C) The claim is accepted for lineage, and the child remains free.
D) The claim is accepted only if the slave provides evidence.

Key Takeaway
The foundling is presumptively free: a claim of slavery requires evidence, but a slave's paternity claim is accepted without affecting the child's freedom, illustrating the Hanafi commitment to protecting the child's welfare and upholding legal certainty.

Default Freedom of a Foundling
Ruling that a claim of ownership over a foundling is rejected without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default legal status of a foundling and identify the condition under which a claim of ownership is accepted.

Core Idea
A foundling is presumed free unless proven otherwise by clear evidence.

In Hanafi fiqh, foundlings (laqit) are given the benefit of the doubt regarding their freedom. This lesson examines a key ruling: a claim of ownership over a foundling is rejected on the basis of this default presumption. The claimant must bring evidence to overcome this presumption. This principle safeguards the child's status and prevents baseless claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and default ruling
The act is claiming ownership of a foundling. The default ruling is that the claim is not accepted (rejected).
2
Understand the basis
The reason given is that the foundling is 'ḥurr ẓāhiran' (outwardly free). This is the default status.
3
Recognize the exception
The claim can be accepted if evidence (bayyinah) is presented. This is the only way to overturn the default.
4
Apply the principle
In any scenario involving a foundling, the default is freedom. A mere assertion of ownership is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنِ ادَّعى أن اللقيط عَبْدَهُ لَمْ يُقْبَلُ مِنْهُ لأنَّهُ حُرِّظَاهِرًا إِلَّا أَنْ يُقِيمَ الْبَيِّنَةَ أَنَّهُ عَبْدُهُ
And whoever claims that the foundling is his slave, it is not accepted from him because he is outwardly free, unless he brings evidence that he is his slave.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling: the default status of a foundling is freedom (hurriyyah). Therefore, any claim of ownership (slavery) is not accepted merely on assertion. The claimant must produce clear evidence (bayyinah) that the child is his slave. This upholds the principle of presuming freedom in matters of lineage and personal status.

Key Terms
حر ظاهراً (ḥurr ẓāhiran): Presumed free based on outward appearance and default ruling.
لقيط (laqīṭ): Foundling; an abandoned child of unknown parentage.
بينة (bayyinah): Clear evidence; legal proof that satisfies the court, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The claimant's mere word
B) The foundling's acknowledgment
C) Evidence (bayyinah)
D) The judge's investigation

Key Takeaway
A foundling is presumed free, and any claim of ownership is rejected unless supported by clear evidence.

Slave's Claim of Paternity Over a Foundling
Ruling that a slave's paternity claim is accepted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's claim of paternity over a foundling and explain why the child's freedom is preserved.

Core Idea
A slave's paternity claim benefits the child, so it is accepted; the child's presumptive freedom is upheld.

In this passage, we see a key distinction in Hanafi law regarding foundlings: while a slave cannot claim ownership of a foundling, he can claim paternity. Why? Because paternity benefits the child by establishing lineage, while ownership would deprive the child of freedom. The principle of 'apparent freedom' (al-ḥurriyyah al-ẓāhiriyyah) is upheld against doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
A slave claims that the foundling is his son. This is a claim of paternity, not ownership.
2
Determine the ruling
Lineage is established from the slave: 'thabata nasabuhu minhu'. The claim is accepted.
3
Understand the reason
Because it benefits the child: 'li-annahu yanfa'uhu'. The claim brings a benefit (nasab) without harm.
Contrast with ownership claim which would harm the child.
4
Note the effect on status
The child remains free: 'wa kana hurran'. The slave's status does not enslave the child.
The mother could be free, so the child is free.
5
Extract the principle
The apparent freedom is not nullified by doubt: 'la tabtul al-hurriyyah al-zahiriyyah bi al-shakk'. The child's freedom is presumed and not overridden by the possibility that the mother might be enslaved.
This is a key Hanafi principle: al-ḥurriyyah al-ẓāhiriyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِن ادَّعَى عَبْدَانَهُ إِبْتُهُ ثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُ مِنْهُ لا نَّهُ يَنْفَعُهُ وَكَانَ حُرَّ الاَنَّ الْمَمْلُوكَ قَدْ تَلِدُ لَهُ الْحُرَّةُ فَلا تَبْطل الحُرّيَّة الظَّاهِرِيَّةُ بِالشَّكِ
If a slave claims that he is his father, his lineage is established from him because it benefits him, and he is free because the free woman may give birth to the slave's child, so the apparent freedom is not nullified by doubt.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
Here, a slave claims that the foundling is his son. Unlike the previous case of ownership, this claim is accepted because it benefits the child (establishes lineage) and does not harm the child's freedom. A slave can father a free child if the mother is free; freedom is presumed. Hence the foundling's apparent freedom (hurriyyah zahirah) is not overturned by the mere possibility that the mother might be a slave. The child's freedom is upheld.

Key Terms
الحرية الظاهرية (al-ḥurriyyah al-ẓāhiriyyah): The default presumption of freedom that is not overridden by doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a slave's claim of paternity over a foundling accepted while a claim of ownership is not?
A) Because the slave is more trustworthy
B) Because it benefits the child and does not affect freedom
C) Because the foundling is always free
D) Because the slave cannot own property

Key Takeaway
A slave's claim of paternity over a foundling is accepted because it benefits the child, and the child remains free due to the presumption of freedom.

Preference of Free over Slave and Muslim over Dhimmi in Claiming a Foundling
Ruling that free and Muslim claimants are preferred over slave and dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the priority rule for claiming a foundling and apply it to competing claimants.

Core Idea
When multiple claimants seek a foundling, free preferred over slave, Muslim over dhimmi — child's welfare is decisive.

In Hanafi law, when a foundling (laqīṭ) is discovered, multiple persons may step forward to claim the child. The default rule is that lineage claims are accepted, but if there is competition, the law applies a hierarchy based on the child's best interest. This lesson focuses on two clear preferences: free over slave, and Muslim over dhimmi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is claiming a foundling (da'wa al-laqīṭ). The ruling is a legal preference: the free person is more entitled (awlā) than the slave, and the Muslim is more entitled than the dhimmi.
2
Understand the rationale
The preference is based on what is more beneficial for the child (tarjīḥan limā huwa al-anẓar fī ḥaqqihi). Being free and Muslim offers greater welfare.
This is an application of istiḥsān based on maṣlaḥah.
3
Apply the rule to competing claimants
Given two claimants, determine which one is free vs slave and Muslim vs dhimmi. The free or Muslim claimant is preferred. If both differences exist, the free Muslim is preferred over a slave dhimmi.
The rule only applies when claimants are of different statuses; if both are free and Muslim, other factors may be considered.
4
Recognize scope
This preference is only for competing claimants. If only one claimant exists, the claim is accepted if valid, regardless of slave or dhimmi status.
The common misunderstanding is that a slave or dhimmi can never claim; they can, but are secondary when there is competition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحُرُّفِي دَعْوَتِهِ اللَّقِيطِ أولى مِنَ الْعَبْدِ وَالْمُسْلِمُ أولى مِنَ اللَّمَى تَرْجِيْحًا لِمَا هُوَ الَا نُظَرُ فِي حَقِهِ
And the free person in his claim of the foundling is more entitled than the slave, and the Muslim is more entitled than the dhimmi, giving preference to what is more beneficial in his right.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
When multiple parties claim lineage of a foundling, preference is given to the free person over a slave, and to the Muslim over a dhimmi. The reason is that the child's best interest (nazar) is prioritized: being free and Muslim is better for the child. This is a practical application of the principle of istihsan based on maslahah.

Key Terms
أولى (awlā): More entitled or preferred in a legal claim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Between a free person and a slave claiming a foundling, who is preferred?
A) The slave
B) The free person
C) Whoever has more wealth
D) The first to make the claim

Key Takeaway
When multiple claimants seek a foundling, the free person is preferred over the slave, and the Muslim over the dhimmi, because the child's welfare (nazar) is the guiding principle.

Luqatah — Found Property

Property Found with the Foundling
Ruling on property attached to a foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on property found attached to a foundling and explain the procedure for spending it on the child.

Core Idea
Property tied to the foundling is the child's, not luqṭah; the finder spends it only with the judge's order.

When a foundling is discovered with money or valuables physically attached (e.g., tied around the neck or on the animal the child is riding), the Hanafi school treats that property as belonging to the child. This lesson clarifies the ruling and the correct procedure for the finder: the property must be spent on the child's needs, but only under the supervision of the judge (qāḍī). A minority view allows spending without judicial permission, but the relied-upon position requires the judge's authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The passage describes property found tied to the foundling. The ruling is that the property is considered the child's (lahu), based on the apparent ownership.
2
Compare with luqṭah (lost property)
Unlike ordinary lost property (luqṭah), which must be announced and cannot be used immediately, this property is immediately treated as the child's because of its physical attachment to the child.
3
Recognize the spending procedure
The finder should spend the property on the child's maintenance (food, clothing, etc.) but must obtain the judge's order. The judge has authority over lost property (māl ḍā'i').
4
Note the difference of opinion
Some Hanafi scholars (qīla) allow spending without the judge's order because the child is the apparent owner. However, the preferred view (muftā bihī) requires the judge's permission to safeguard the child's interests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ وُجِدَ مَعَ اللقِيطِ مَالٌ مَشدُودٌ عَلَيْهِ فَهُوَلَهُ اِعْتِبَارُ اللطَّاهِرِ وَ كَذَا إِذَا كَانَ مَشْدُودُ اعَلَى دَابَّةٍ وَهُوَ عَلَيْهَا لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا ثُمَّ يَصْرِفُهُ الْوَاجِدَ إِلَيْهِ بِأَمْرِ الْقَاضِي لِأَنَّهُ مَالٌ ضَائِعٌ وَلِلْقَاضِي وَلَايَةٌ صَرْفِ مِثْلِهِ إِلَيْهِ وَقِيلَ يَصْرِفُهُ بِغَيْرِ أمْرِ الْقَاضِي لَانَّهُ اللَّقِيْطُ ظَاهِرًا
And if property is found with the foundling tied to him, it is his, considering the apparent. Likewise if it is tied to an animal on which he is found, for what we mentioned. Then the finder spends it on him by order of the judge, because it is lost property, and the judge has authority to spend such property on him. And it is said he spends it without the judge's order, because he is the foundling apparently.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This ruling deals with property physically attached to the foundling (e.g., money tied around his neck or on the animal he sits on). Such property is presumed to belong to the child, not to the finder. The finder should spend it on the child's needs (food, clothing) but must do so with the judge's permission because the property is considered lost (ḍā'i') and the judge is the guardian of lost property. There is a difference of opinion: some say the finder can spend without judicial permission because the apparent ownership gives him authority, but the relied-upon view is that the judge's order is required.

Key Terms
مال ضائع (māl ḍā'i'): Property that has no apparent owner; here it is presumed the foundling's but still requires judicial oversight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How should the finder use the property found with the foundling?
A) Keep it for himself
B) Spend it on the child with judge's permission
C) Give it to charity
D) Announce it as lost property

Key Takeaway
Property physically attached to a foundling belongs to the child, and the finder must spend it on the child under judicial supervision.

Finder's Authority to Spend on Foundling's Necessities
Ruling that the finder can spend on necessities for the foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of the finder's authority to spend on a foundling's maintenance.

Core Idea
The finder may spend on necessities only — food and clothing — not on luxuries or investments.

This lesson clarifies the legal authority (wilāyah) of a person who finds an abandoned child (laqīṭ). The ruling is simple but precise: the finder may spend from the child's own property only on essential needs. This is part of the finder's custodial duty until the child matures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is spending on the foundling's needs. The ruling is that the finder has the authority (wilāyah) to do so.
2
Identify the scope
The authority covers only necessities, exemplified by food (ṭa'ām) and clothing (kiswah).
3
Understand the basis
The basis is that spending on necessities is part of the caretaker's duty of maintenance (infāq).
4
Note the limitation
The authority does not extend to non-essential purchases or investments without judicial authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَهُ وَلَايَةُ الَّا نُفَاقِ وَشِرَاءُ مَالَا بُدَّلَـهُ مِـنْـهُ كَالطَّعَامِ وَالْكِسْوَةِ لِأَنَّهُ مِنَ الْإِنْفَاقِ لَـهُ
And he has the authority of spending and buying what is necessary for him, like food and clothing, because it is part of spending for him.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This confirms that the finder (multaqiṭ) has the legal capacity (wilāyah) to spend from the foundling's property on essential needs: food (ṭa'ām) and clothing (kiswah). These are considered necessities (mā lā budda minhu). The authority is limited to essential maintenance, not discretionary purchases. This ruling is based on the principle that the finder is the caretaker until the child's maturity.

Key Terms
ولاية الإنفاق (wilāyat al-infiāq): Legal authority to spend on someone's maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What can the finder buy for the foundling using his property?
A) Luxury items
B) Food and clothing
C) Property for investment
D) Gifts for others

Key Takeaway
The finder's spending authority is strictly limited to essential maintenance items such as food and clothing.

Guardianship and Marriage of the Foundling
Ruling statement on marriage guardianship of foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling prohibiting the finder from marrying the foundling and explain the three causes of guardianship required for marriage validity.

Core Idea
Without kinship, ownership, or authority, the finder has no right to arrange the foundling's marriage.

This lesson presents a concise ruling from Hanafi fiqh: the finder of a foundling (laqīṭ) cannot contract a marriage for the child using the foundling's own property. The reason is the complete absence of any valid basis for guardianship (wilāyah). Understanding this rule protects the foundling's welfare and clarifies that a caretaker is not automatically a guardian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
State the central ruling: 'It is not permissible for the finder to marry the foundling.' The subject is the finder (multaqiṭ) and the act of arranging marriage for the foundling.
The ruling is a prohibition (ḥaram) because it is stated as la yajūz.
2
List the three bases of guardianship
Memorize the three causes of wilāyah: qarābah (kinship by blood), milk (ownership, e.g., a master over a slave), and sulṭanah (authority of the ruler or judge). These are the only legitimate grounds for acting as a marriage guardian.
These three are exclusive in the Hanafi school; no other relationship suffices.
3
Apply the rule to the finder
Explain why the finder lacks each basis: he is not a relative (no nasab), he does not own the foundling (the child is free), and he has no sulṭanah (the judge retains that authority). Therefore, his attempt at marriage is invalid.
The text explicitly states 'li-‘adam sabab al-wilāyah' – due to the nonexistence of the cause.
4
State the practical consequence
If the finder nonetheless arranges a marriage for the foundling, the contract is void (bāṭil) because it lacks a necessary condition (a valid guardian). The judge must appoint a proper guardian or act himself.
This ruling protects the foundling from exploitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجُوزُ تَزْوِيجُ المُلْتَقَط لانعِدَامٍ سَبَبِ الوِلَايَةِ مِنَ القَرَابَةِ وَالْمِلْكِ وَالسُّلْطَنَةِ
And it is not permissible to marry the foundling because of the absence of the cause of guardianship: kinship, ownership, or authority.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
The finder has no right to arrange marriage for the foundling using the foundling's property. Guardianship (wilāyah) in marriage requires one of three bases: close kinship (e.g., father, grandfather), ownership (master for slave), or governmental authority (sulṭān). Since the finder has none, he cannot act as marriage guardian. The marriage contract would be invalid. This protects the foundling's interests until a proper guardian is appointed by the judge.

Key Terms
سبب الولاية (sabab al-wilāyah): The legal basis for guardianship, here limited to kinship, ownership, or sultanic authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible for the finder to marry the foundling?
A) Because the foundling is too young to marry.
B) Because the finder lacks any of the three causes of guardianship: kinship, ownership, or authority.
C) Because the finder must use his own property, not the foundling's.
D) Because the judge must always perform the marriage of a foundling.

Key Takeaway
A finder of a foundling cannot arrange his/her marriage because he lacks the necessary legal guardianship (wilāyah), which requires kinship, ownership, or authority.
